Impossibility of Increasing Both Opposites Simultaneously
Refutation of deviation by opposite qualities simultaneously.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why deviation from balanced temperament cannot involve an increase of two opposite qualities at the same time, and the subtle exception involving elemental masses.

Core Idea
Inclination toward one opposite precludes simultaneous increase in its opposite.

In the study of temperaments, one might wonder whether it is possible to deviate from balance by increasing both heat and cold together. The author addresses this question with a clear logical argument, showing it is impossible unless we shift our focus from qualities to quantities. This passage refutes a potential objection and clarifies a key distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to see the author's concise style. Then read the translation to get the full meaning.
Pay attention to the logical flow: the first sentence introduces the limitation to eight temperaments.
2
Understand the key argument
The author explains that deviation from balance toward heat means heat exceeds cold. Therefore, cold cannot simultaneously be in excess. Grasp this logical point.
This is the core refutation of the possibility of two opposite qualities increasing together.
3
Identify the objection being refuted
Some claimed that three qualities could increase, adding four new temperaments. The author shows this is contradictory unless we consider elemental masses rather than qualities.
The exception is a subtle but important nuance.
4
Apply the distinction between qualities and masses
Understand that the argument hinges on qualities (kayfiyyat) vs. elemental masses (ajram). When we talk about masses, it is possible to increase both hot and cold masses together, but not their qualities.
This distinction prevents misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (أو) كيفيتين غير متضادتين فينحصر في ثمانية. يشير إلى أنه لا يمكن الخروج عن الاعتدال بالحرارة والبرودة جميعاً أو بالرطوبة واليبوسة جميعاً، لأن الميل عن حاق الوسط إلى الحرارة مثلاً، معناه زيادة الحرارة على البرودة، فكيف يتصور مع ذلك زيادة البرودة على الحرارة؟ وبهذا يبطل ما قيل: يجوز الخروج بكيفيات ثلاث، فيزيد على الأقسام الثمانية أربعة أخرى هي المزاج الحار البارد والرطب أو اليابس، والمزاج الرطب اليابس الحار أو البارد. نعم يتصور ذلك لو اشترط في المعتدل تساوي أجرام العناصر أيضاً، بأن يزيد جرم الحار والبارد جميعاً أو الرطب واليابس جميعاً.
He said: '(Or) two non-opposing qualities, so it is limited to eight.' He indicates that it is not possible to exit from balance by heat and cold together, or by moisture and dryness together, because the inclination from the exact middle to heat, for example, means an increase of heat over cold; so how can one imagine with that an increase of cold over heat? And with this is refuted what was said: 'Exit by three qualities is permissible, so there would be added to the eight divisions four others: the hot-cold and moist or dry temperament, and the moist-dry and hot or cold temperament.' Yes, that can be imagined if in the balanced [temperament] equality of the elemental masses is also stipulated, such that the mass of hot and cold both increase, or that of moist and dry both increase.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage refutes a potential objection regarding the eight temperaments. The author first quotes the statement that deviation from balance occurs by two non-opposing qualities, limiting the number of complexions to eight. Then he explains that it is impossible to deviate from balance by both heat and cold at the same time, because deviation toward heat means heat exceeds cold, so cold cannot also be in excess. He uses this to refute a claim that three qualities could be simultaneously increased, which would add four more complexions (e.g., hot-cold-moist). He then notes an exception: if we stipulate equality of the elemental masses (ajram al-'anasir), then it is conceivable to increase both hot and cold masses together (or both moist and dry). This subtle point shows the argument is about qualities (kayfiyyat) rather than quantities (ajram).

Key Terms
كيفيتين غير متضادتين
اعتدال
حرارة
برودة
رطوبة
يبوسة
أجرام العناصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the refutation applies universally, making it impossible to increase both heat and cold in any way.
Correction: The refutation applies only to qualities (kayfiyyat), not to elemental masses (ajram). If we consider masses, it is possible to have both hot and cold masses increase together while maintaining balance.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that one cannot deviate from balance by both heat and cold simultaneously?
A) Because heat and cold are not qualities.
B) Because deviation toward heat means heat exceeds cold, so cold cannot also be in excess.
C) Because the number of temperaments would exceed eight.
D) Because it is impossible for two opposite qualities to exist together in any case.

Key Takeaway
Deviation from balance cannot involve increasing two opposite qualities simultaneously, but the same is possible for elemental masses if their equality is maintained.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you desire two opposite outcomes (e.g., wanting both comfort and deep spiritual growth, which requires struggle). How does this lesson's principle—that you cannot increase both opposites simultaneously in terms of qualities—reveal an inner contradiction you must resolve? What first step will you take today to choose one or to seek a balanced increase in the underlying intention?
?
The passage distinguishes between qualities and masses: opposites can sometimes coexist if considered from a different perspective. In your own spiritual journey, are you confusing outward actions (qualities) with inward states (masses)? For instance, do you judge yourself based on visible deeds while ignoring the intention behind them? Commit to one act this week that aligns your inner intention with your outer action.
?
The author allows an exception when equality is maintained. Name one relationship or habit where you try to hold two opposing attitudes (e.g., being humble but also assertive) in a way that may be possible if they are balanced. What concrete change can you make to ensure this balance, and by when will you start?

The Balanced Temperament: Definition, Types, and Two Meanings
Sanusi defines balanced temperament and distinguishes true vs. medical balance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the balanced temperament, its classification by type, species, individual, and organ, and the distinction between the true (hypothetical) and medical (practical) balanced temperament.

Core Idea
Balance means giving each thing its fitting share—not always equal mixture.

This passage from Sanusi's Sharh al-Maqasid unpacks the concept of a 'balanced temperament' in Islamic medicine and natural philosophy. The author distinguishes between a pure theoretical equality and a practical proportionality that suits each creature's nature and actions. We'll see how this applies to species, individuals, and even organs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and define balance
First, read the Arabic and English sides. Notice how the author begins by defining the balanced temperament as giving the mixture its proper share of quantities and qualities.
The term 'al-qist' (portion) is key—it means fitting share, not necessarily equality.
2
Trace the classification
Follow the levels: type (naw'), species (sinf), individual (shakhs), and organ ('udw). See how each has its own suitable temperament.
The example of the human shows degrees between extremes.
3
Compare the two meanings of 'balanced'
The passage contrasts the true balanced (al-mu'tadil al-haqiqi) derived from equality, with the medical balanced (al-mu'tadil al-tibbi) derived from just division. Understand why each matters.
The medical balance is what we encounter in real creatures—like lions and rabbits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال المعتدل: كما يتوفر فيه على الممتزج القسط الذي ينبغي له من الكميات والكيفيات نوعاً أو صنفاً أو شخصاً، أو عضواً كل بحسب الخارج أو الداخل بمعنى. أن للإنسان مثلاً مزاجاً هو أليق الأمزجة به، بالنسبة إلى سائر الأنواع، له مراتب بين طرفيه: أحدهما: أقربهما إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي، وكذا للتركي بالنسبة إلى سائر الأصناف، وإلى أفراده ولزيد بالنسبة إلى سائر الأشخاص وإلى أحواله وللقلب كذلك. فعرض مزاج النوع يشتمل على أمزجة إضافية، والصنف على أشخاصية، والشخص على أحواليته، واعتبار العضو إنما هو من جهة أن الطب ينظر بالبوق وفي الأعضاء، وإلا فهو نوع برأسه، كالإنسان ينبغي أن يكون له الاعتبارات الثلاث، واعتدال الشخص يعتبر بحسب تكافؤ قوى أعضائه، حتى يحصل من المجموع ما يقرب إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي، ولا خفاء في أنه ليس هناك امتزاج أجزاء، وحصول كيفية واحدة، فكأنه مجرد وضع، وإضافة، أو كيفيات لجميع الأعضاء من حيث يتأثر بعضها عن البعض بمجرد المجاورة من غير امتزاج. المعتدل الحقيقي مشتقاً من التعادل بمعنى التساوي، وهذا هو ما مر. وكان هو المعتدل الفرضي والطبي المستعمل في صناعة الطب مشتقاً من العدل في القسمة، ومعناه المزاج الذي قد تؤثر فيه على الممتزج من كميات العناصر وكيفياتها القسط له، ويليق بحاله، ويكون أنسب بأفعاله مثلاً شأن الأسد الجرأة الذي ينبغي والاقدام وشأن الأرنب الخوف والجبن فيليق بالأول غلبة الحرارة، وبالثاني غلبة البرودة، وهذا الاعتدال يعرض له ثمانية اعتبارات، لأن أليقية المزاج للممتزج. إما أن تكون بحسب الأفعال المطلوبة من النوع، أو من المصنف أو من
It may be said: The balanced [temperament] is such that the mixture receives the portion (al-qist) that befits it of quantities and qualities, whether by type, species, individual, or organ, each according to external or internal. Meaning: For the human, for example, there is a temperament that is the most suitable temperament for him, relative to all other species; it has degrees between its two extremes: one of them is the closest to the true balanced temperament. Likewise for the composite relative to all species, and for its individuals, and for Zayd relative to all persons and to his states, and for the heart likewise. Thus the broad temperament of the type includes additional temperaments, and the species includes individual temperaments, and the individual includes state-based temperaments. The consideration of the organ is only in that medicine looks at the pulse (?) and at the organs; otherwise it is a type in itself, just as the human should have the three considerations. The balance of the individual is considered by the equivalence of the powers of his organs, so that from the totality something close to true balance results. It is no secret that there is no mixture of parts and attainment of a single quality there; it is rather a mere position, addition, or qualities of all the organs in that some are affected by others through mere adjacency without mixture. The true balanced [temperament] is derived from 'al-ta'adul' meaning equality, and that is what has passed. And it is the hypothetical balanced [temperament], and the medical [temperament] used in the art of medicine is derived from 'al-'adl' in division. Its meaning is the temperament that imparts to the mixture the portion (al-qist) of the quantities and qualities of the elements that fits it, suits its state, and is most appropriate for its actions. For example, the affair of the lion is courage and daring, which require [heat?]; and the affair of the rabbit is fear and cowardice, so for the first a predominance of heat is suitable, and for the second a predominance of cold. This balance has eight considerations, because the suitability of the temperament for the mixture is either according to the actions required by the type, or by the species, or by
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed definition and classification of the balanced temperament (al-mu'tadil). It begins by defining the balanced temperament as that which gives the mixture (al-mumtazij) the proper share (al-qist) of quantities (kammiyyat) and qualities (kayfiyyat) appropriate for its type (naw'), species (sinf), individual (shakhs), or organ ('udw). It then elaborates with the example of humans, noting that there is a temperament most suitable for the human species relative to others, with degrees between extremes. It extends this to composite bodies (al-turki), individuals like Zayd, and even organs like the heart. The broad temperament of the type encompasses more specific temperaments. The consideration of organs is relevant to medicine—likely referring to diagnosis by pulse (al-bawq is uncertain) and examination of organs. The balance of an individual is determined by the equilibrium of his organs' powers, resulting in an overall nearness to true balance. There is no actual mixture of elements in that case; it is a 'juxtaposition' (mujarrad wad') of qualities. The passage then distinguishes two meanings of 'balanced': (1) the true balanced temperament (al-mu'tadil al-haqiqi), derived from 'ta'adul' meaning equality—this is the hypothetical ideal; (2) the medical balanced temperament (al-mu'tadil al-tibbi), derived from 'adl' meaning just division, which is the temperament that imparts the appropriate share to the mixture, suiting its state and actions. Examples: lions need heat, rabbits need cold. This medical balance has eight considerations based on whether the suitability concerns actions of the type, species, etc. The text cuts off at this point, but it has established the distinction and the types of considerations.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Balanced temperament always means an equal mixture of elements.
Correction: The passage shows balance often means a proportional fitness suited to the entity, not equal parts. True equality is only hypothetical; medical balance is a just division fitting the creature's nature.

Checkpoint
What is the key difference between the 'true balanced temperament' (al-mu'tadil al-haqiqi) and the 'medical balanced temperament' (al-mu'tadil al-tibbi) according to this passage?
A) The true one is impossible to achieve, while the medical one is actual.
B) The true one is derived from 'equality' (al-ta'adul), while the medical one is derived from 'justice' (al-'adl) in division.
C) The true one applies only to humans, while the medical one applies to all animals.
D) They are the same thing.

Key Takeaway
The balanced temperament is not a single equal mixture but a fitting proportion for each entity, with a theoretical ideal and practical medical applications.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of 'balance' in your own life—your time, relationships, or worship—do you assume it means equal distribution, or have you ever considered that balance might mean giving each thing its due portion, even if unequal? Which mindset are you actually living by, and does it need to change?
?
The passage says a rabbit's fitting temperament includes fear and cold; a lion's includes heat and courage. What is the 'rabbit' or 'lion' in your character that you have been trying to force into a different shape, expecting it to act against its given nature? How will you honor the temperament Allah has placed in you today?
?
Sanusi distinguishes between an ideal perfect balance and a practical balance that fits your specific state. Are you chasing an impossible ideal of perfection that leaves you paralyzed, or are you accepting the fitting balance for your current circumstances? What would change if you truly embraced the medical balance for your soul right now?

Types of Temperamental Moderation and Their Eight Considerations
Explains two kinds of moderation and their application across species, varieties, individuals, and organs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between true and medical moderation and how the eight considerations of suitability arise from different levels (species, variety, person, organ) and directions (external/internal).

Core Idea
Medical moderation is relative suitability, not absolute equality.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid dives into the nuanced concept of temperamental moderation. It clarifies that the 'moderate' in medicine is not a fixed ideal but a proportional suitability for each entity. The author then systematically breaks down the eight considerations of this medical moderation, showing how it applies across species, varieties, individuals, and organs, both externally and internally.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of moderation
The passage first introduces two kinds: true moderation (i'tidal haqiqi) based on equality, and medical moderation (i'tidal tibbi) based on suitability. Understand their derivations and applications.
True moderation is hypothetical and unattainable; medical moderation is practical and relative.
2
Learn the levels of temperament
Temperament is considered at four levels: species, variety (subspecies), individual person, and organ. Each level has its own 'relative temperaments' (amzija idafiyya).
3
Understand the eight considerations
Medical moderation has eight considerations arising from four categories (species, variety, person, organ) each evaluated in two directions: external (relative to others) and internal (relative to its own components).
This yields 4 x 2 = 8 distinct considerations.
4
Examine the human example
The passage uses the human species to illustrate: a specific range of heat (10-20) defines humanity; beyond it becomes another species. The 'best' temperament within that range is called the canon (dustur), found in one exceptional individual.
Similar logic applies to variety (e.g., Turk) and person (e.g., Zayd).
5
Reflect on the concept of suitability
Medical moderation is about what is 'most suitable' for a given entity, not about a universal mean. This is crucial for understanding how temperament theory applies in medicine and philosophy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما : أقربهما إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي، وكذا للتركي بالنسبة إلى سائر الأصناف، وإلى أفراده ولزيد بالنسبة إلى سائر الأشخاص وإلى أحواله وللقلب كذلك . فعرض مزاج النوع يشتمل على أمزجة إضافية، والصنف على أشخاصية، والشخص على أحوالية، واعتبار العضو إنما هو من جهة أن الطب ينظر بالبوق وفي الأعضاء، وإلا فهو نوع برأسه، كالإنسان ينبغي أن يكون له الاعتبارات الثلاث، واعتدال الشخص يعتبر بحسب تكافؤ قوى أعضائه، حتى يحصل من المجموع ما يقرب إلى الاعتدال الحقيقي، ولا خفاء في أنه ليس هناك امتزاج أجزاء، وحصول كيفية واحدة، فكأنه مجرد وضع، وإضافة، أو كيفيات لجميع الأعضاء من حيث يتأثر بعضها عن البعض بمجرد المجاورة من غير امتزاج . المعتدل الحقيقي مشتقاً من التعادل بمعنى التساوي، وهذا هو ما مر كان هو المعتدل الفرضي والطبي المستعمل في صناعة الطب مشتقاً من العدل في القسمة، ومعناه المزاج الذي قد تؤثر فيه على الممتزج من كميات العناصر وكيفياتها القسط له، ويليق بحاله، ويكون أنسب بأفعاله مثلاً شأن الأسد الجرأة الذي ينبغي والاقدام وشأن الأرنب الخوف والجبن فيليق بالأول غلبة الحرارة، وبالثاني غلبة البرودة، وهذا الاعتدال يعرض له ثمانية اعتبارات، لأن أليقية المزاج للممتزج. إما أن تكون بحسب الأفعال المطلوبة من النوع، أو من المصنف أو من الشخص، أو من العضو، وكل من ذلك يعتبر إما بالقياس إلى الخارج، أعني للنوع إلى سائر الأنواع، وللصنف إلى سائر الأصناف من ذلك النوع، وللشخص إلى سائر الأشخاص من ذلك الصنف، وللعضو إلى سائر الأعضاء البدن، وإما بالقياس إلى الداخل. أعني للنوع إلى ما له من الأصناف، وللصنف إلى ما له من الأشخاص، وللشخص إلى ما يعرض له من الأحوال، وكذا للعضو مثلاً للبدن الإنساني مزاج هو أليق به من حيث أنه إنسان من مزاج أي نوع فرض، بحيث إذا تغير أو فسد اختلت الأفعال المختصة بهذا النوع، وله مراتب يتردد فيها بين طرفي إفراط وتفريط يعبر عنها بسعة المزاج، للقطع بأن ليس أفراد الإنسان على مزاج واحد، وليس أيضاً كل مزاج صالحاً للصورة الإنسانية، فلنفرض أن حرارته لا تزيد على عشرين، ولا تنقص عن عشرة، بل تتردد بينهما، فإذا زادت على عشرين لم يكن الممتزج إنساناً، بل فرساً، وإذا نقصت من عشرة لم يكن إنساناً، بل أرنباً، ثم لا محالة تكون هناك واسطة بين هذين الطرفين. أعني الإفراط والتفريط هي أليق به من حيث أنه إنسان من مزاج. أي فرد فرض من أفراد الإنسان، ويكون أفضل أمزجة الإنسان وأقربها إلى الاعتدال في سن بلغ فيه النشو غايته، وهو وإن لم يكن الاعتدال لكنه الحقيقي الذي حكموا بامتناع وجوده يعز وجوده، إذ لا يوجد إلا في شخص واحد تجعله الأطباء دستوراً، يقاس إليه سائر الأشخاص، وكذا للتركي مزاج خاص هو أليق به من حيث أنه تركي في أمزجة سائر أصناف الإنسان له عرض أي سعة لو خرج الشخص عنها لم يكن تركياً، بل صنفاً آخر وله واسطة هي أليق به من فرد فرض من أفراد التركي أفضل أمزجة الصنف، وإن لم يلزم أن تكون أفضل أمزجة النوع، وكذا لزيد مزاج هو أليق به من حيث هو هذا الشخص المعين، أي أنسب بالصفات المختصة
One of them: the closer of the two to true equilibrium, and likewise for the Turk relative to the rest of the varieties, and to its individuals, and for Zayd relative to the rest of the persons and to his states, and for the heart similarly. So the temperament of the species encompasses relative temperaments, and the [temperament of the] variety encompasses individual [temperaments], and the [temperament of the] person encompasses state [temperaments], and the consideration of the organ is only from the aspect that medicine examines by the tube and in the organs, otherwise it is a type by itself, like the human being should have the three considerations, and the moderation of the person is considered according to the balance of the powers of his organs, until from the sum what approximates true equilibrium obtains, and it is no secret that there is no mixing of parts and obtaining a single quality, so it is as if it is a mere positing and relation, or qualities of all the organs in terms of some being affected by others through mere contiguity without mixing. The true moderate is derived from at-ta'adul meaning equality, and this is what was the hypothetical moderate, and the medical moderate used in the art of medicine is derived from justice in division, and its meaning is the temperament which has an effect on the compound from the quantities of the elements and their qualities that is its equitable share, befitting its state, and most suitable for its actions, for example the nature of the lion is the courage that is appropriate and boldness, and the nature of the rabbit is fear and cowardice, so the first befits predominance of heat, and the second befits predominance of cold. This moderation has eight considerations, because the suitability of the temperament for the compound: either according to the actions required from the species, or from the variety, or from the person, or from the organ, and each of these is considered either relative to what is external, i.e., for the species relative to the rest of the species, and for the variety relative to the rest of the varieties of that species, and for the person relative to the rest of the persons of that variety, and for the organ relative to the rest of the organs of the body, or relative to what is internal, i.e., for the species relative to what it has of varieties, and for the variety relative to what it has of persons, and for the person relative to what occurs to him of states, and likewise for the organ. For example, the human body has a temperament that is more suitable for it in terms of being human than the temperament of any assumed type, such that if it changes or corrupts, the actions specific to this type become impaired, and it has degrees fluctuating between two extremes of excess and deficiency, which are expressed as the capacity of the temperament, because it is certain that individuals of humans are not on one temperament, and also not every temperament is suitable for the human form. Let us assume that its heat does not exceed twenty, nor fall below ten, but fluctuates between them. So if it exceeds twenty, the compound is not human, but a horse; and if it falls below ten, it is not human, but a rabbit. Then inevitably there is a middle between these two extremes, i.e., excess and deficiency, that is more suitable for it in terms of being human than the temperament of any assumed individual of humans, and it is the best of human temperaments and the closest to moderation in the age when growth has reached its peak, and although it is not moderation, it is the real [moderation] which they ruled its existence impossible, as its existence is rare, for it exists only in one person whom physicians make a canon against whom all other persons are measured. Likewise, the Turk has a specific temperament that is more suitable for him in terms of being Turkish among the temperaments of the rest of the varieties of humans; it has a breadth, i.e., a capacity, such that if a person departs from it he would not be Turkish, but another variety, and it has a middle that is more suitable for him than any assumed individual of the Turks, the best temperament of the variety, though not necessarily the best temperament of the species. Likewise, Zayd has a temperament that is more suitable for him in terms of being this specific person, i.e., most appropriate to the specific qualities ...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed classification of temperamental moderation and its applications. It begins by distinguishing two types of moderation: one closer to true equilibrium (i'tidal haqiqi) and one used in medicine (i'tidal tibbi). The first is purely conceptual and practically unattainable; the second is relative suitability based on the specific object. The passage then explains that the temperament of a species encompasses relative temperaments (amzija idafiyya), the variety encompasses individual temperaments (ashkhasiyya), and the person encompasses state temperaments (ahwaliyya). The organ is considered separately because medicine examines it, but it is a type in itself. The moderation of a person is based on the balance of his organs' powers such that the sum approximates true equilibrium, even though there is no actual mixing of parts—only mutual influence through contiguity. The true moderate is derived from equality (ta'adul), while the medical moderate derives from just division (adl fi al-qisma), meaning the temperament's effect on the compound is proportionate and suitable for its actions. Examples are given: the lion's boldness suits heat predominance, the rabbit's timidity suits cold. This medical moderation has eight considerations, arising from the suitability of temperament either by the required actions of species, variety, person, or organ, and each is considered either externally (relative to other species/varieties/persons/organs) or internally (relative to its own subspecies/persons/states). The human body is used as an example: there is a range of heat (10 to 20) within which humanity is maintained; beyond it, the compound becomes another species. Within this range, there is a best temperament for the species, though it exists only in one person (the canon). Similarly, the Turkish variety has a specific range with a best temperament for that variety, and Zayd as an individual has a temperament most suitable for his specific qualities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing the purely hypothetical true moderation with the practical medical moderation; also assuming that one type of moderation applies uniformly across species, variety, and individual.
Correction: True moderation is an abstract ideal of equality, while medical moderation is a relative suitability that varies across levels and directions. Each level (species, variety, person, organ) has its own appropriate temperament.

Checkpoint
How many considerations does medical moderation have according to this passage?
A) Four
B) Six
C) Eight
D) Ten

Key Takeaway
Medical moderation is context-dependent suitability, varying across levels and directions, not a single absolute standard.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you chasing an impossible 'true moderation' in your own life, seeking perfect balance in everything, even while the lesson shows that absolute equality doesn't exist? What would it mean for you to accept a 'suitable' temperament for who you are right now?
?
The Turk has a temperament that suits his nature, and Zayd has his own. When have you tried to force yourself into a mold that wasn't made for you—whether in personality, work, or worship—because you believed there's one 'right' way? How can you honor the unique temperament Allah has given you?
?
Physicians used a single person as a canon to measure others. Who are the people in your life whose 'temperament' you envy or try to imitate? Are you willing to stop comparing and instead discover the middle that is most suitable for your own state—even if it's not the 'best' in someone else's eyes?

Breadth of Temperament for the Individual and the Heart
Illustrates breadth of temperament through examples of an individual and an organ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the temperament of an individual and an organ like the heart has a breadth with two extremes and an optimal midpoint.

Core Idea
Temperament breadth is the range that allows life and health, with a best point.

The concept of temperament breadth is crucial for understanding health and disease. This passage uses the examples of Zayd's changing temperament with age and the heart's specific temperament to illustrate that each entity has a permissible range and an ideal middle state.

Guided Path
1
Read about Zayd's temperament breadth
Notice that Zayd's temperament changes as he ages, and it has two extremes (excess and deficiency) that cannot be exceeded. There is also a midpoint that is best for him at that time.
The midpoint is relative to his age.
2
Read about the heart's temperament breadth
The heart also has a temperament that is most suitable for it, with two limits and a midpoint. If it exceeds the limits, it is no longer a heart.
3
Compare individual and organ breadth
Both examples show that each entity has a defined range and an optimal state within that range.

به من أمزجة أفراد ذلك الصنف (۱) ، وهو المزاج الذي يجب أن يكون زيد عليه، ليكون موجوداً حياً صحيحاً. ثم لاخفاء في أن له سعة ضرورة أن مزاجه وهو شاب غیر مزاجه وهو شيخ أو صبي أو كهل، ولها طرفا إفراط وتفريط لا تتعداهما ضرورة أن ليس كل مزاج صالحاً له مع اختصاصه بمزاج معين وبينهما واسطة إذا حصلت لزيد كان على أفضل ما ينبغي أن يكون عليه بمعنى أن المزاج الذي له في ذلك الوقت أصلح لأفعاله من المزاج الذي له في سائر (۳) أوقاته ، وكذا للقلب مزاج هو أليق به من أمزجة سائر أعضاء البدن . عريض له طرفان إذا تجاوزهما لم يكن القلب،واسطة إذا حصلت للقلب كان على أفضل ما ينبغي أن يكون عليه، فظهر أن عرض مزاج النوع يشتمل على أمزجة أصنافه، لأن غرض (۳) الصنف، بعض غرض (٤) النوع ، وغرض مزاج الصنف يشتمل على أمزجة أشخاصه،وعرض مزاج الشخص على أمزجته في حالاته، وليس مزاج العضو داخلاً في العروض المتقدمة(٥) ، لأنها مأخوذة باعتبار مجموع البدن ، من حيث هو مجموع إذا تكافأت الأعضاء الحارة بالباردة والرطبة ،باليابسة، فيستحيل أن يكون مزاج مجموع البدن ، مزاج عضو واحد فإن قيل : العضو نوع من أنواع الكائنات مشتمل على أصناف مشتملة على أشخاص، فينبغي أن يعتبر يعتبر له اعتدال نوعي وصنفي (٦) وشخصي كل منها بالقياس إلى الداخل والخارج دون أن يجعل قسم (۷) برأسه مقابلاً لها . قلنا : نعم إلا أنهم نظروا إلى أن الطب (1) ينظر في أحوال بدن الانسان وأعضائه من حيث كونها على اعتدالها، أو خارجة عنه، واعتبار مزاج البدن، إنما هو باعتبار تكافؤ أعضائه وتعادلها في المزاج ، بأن تكون حرارة ما هو حار منها كالقلب تعادل برودة ما هو بارد منها كالدماغ ، ويبوسة ما هو يابس منها كالعظم تعادل رطوبة ما هو رطب منها كالكبد ، بحيث إذا نسب جميع ما في البدن من الحرارة إلى جميع ما فيه من البرودة كان قريباً (۱) من التساوي، وكذا الرطوبة مع اليبوسة. وبالجملة يكون الحاصل من المجموع قريباً من الاعتدال الحقيقي. ثم لاخفاء في أنه ليس ههنا أعني في مزاج جملة البدن المعبر بالمزاج الشخصي، اختلاط أجزاء (۲) الأعضاء، وتصغرها وتمارسها ، وكأنه مجرد وضع ، وإضافة للبعض إلى البعض أو كيفيات تحصل لجميع الأعضاء من جهة تأثر بعضها من البعض بمجرد المجاورة من غير امتزاج واختلاط للأجزاء .
From the temperaments of the individuals of that class (1), which is the temperament that Zayd must have in order to exist alive and healthy. Then it is not hidden that it has breadth necessarily, because his temperament when he is a youth is not his temperament when he is an old man, or a boy, or a middle-aged man, and it has two extremes of excess and deficiency which it does not exceed, necessarily because not every temperament is suitable for him, with its being specific to a particular temperament, and between them is a middle point; if it is attained by Zayd, he is in the best state he should be, meaning that the temperament he has at that time is more suitable for his actions than the temperament he has at other times (3). Similarly, the heart has a temperament that is more fitting to it than the temperaments of the rest of the body's organs. It has breadth with two limits; if they are exceeded, it is not the heart, and a middle point; if attained by the heart, it is in the best state it should be. Thus it appears that the breadth of the temperament of the species includes the temperaments of its classes, because the purpose (3) of the class is part of the purpose (4) of the species, and the breadth of the temperament of the class includes the temperaments of its individuals, and the breadth of the temperament of the individual includes his temperaments in his states, and the temperament of an organ is not included in the aforementioned breadths (5), because they are taken with consideration of the entire body, insofar as it is a whole, when the hot organs are balanced with the cold, and the moist with the dry, so it is impossible for the temperament of the entire body to be the temperament of a single organ. For it is said: An organ is a type of types of beings, comprising classes comprising individuals, so it should be considered for it a generic, specific (6), and individual equilibrium, each relative to the internal and external, without making it a separate division (7) opposing them. We say: Yes, but they looked at that medicine (1) considers the states of the human body and its organs insofar as they are in equilibrium or outside of it, and the consideration of the body's temperament is only with consideration of the balance of its organs and their equality in temperament, such that the heat of what is hot among them, like the heart, balances the cold of what is cold among them, like the brain, and the dryness of what is dry among them, like bone, balances the moisture of what is moist among them, like the liver, such that if all the heat in the body is compared to all the cold in it, it is close (1) to equality, and likewise moisture with dryness. In summary, the resultant from the whole is close to real equilibrium. Then it is not hidden that here, i.e., in the temperament of the entire body, expressed as the personal temperament, there is no mingling of the parts (2) of the organs and their becoming small and mixing, but rather it is merely a position and a relation of some to others, or qualities that occur to all the organs from the influence of some on others by mere juxtaposition without mingling and mixing of parts.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of the breadth (عرض) of temperament (مزاج) across different levels: species, class, individual, and organ. It begins by noting that a person's temperament changes with age (youth, old age, childhood) and has a range with two extremes and a midpoint. Similarly, each organ, like the heart, has its own suitable temperament with limits. The author then generalizes: the breadth of the species' temperament encompasses the temperaments of its classes, and the breadth of a class encompasses its individuals. However, the temperament of an individual organ is not included in the earlier-mentioned breadths, because those consider the whole body as a balance of hot, cold, moist, and dry organs. An objection is raised: since an organ is also a type with classes and individuals, it should have its own generic, specific, and individual equilibrium. The answer clarifies that medicine (الطب) considers the whole body's equilibrium as a balance of all organs, not the mixing of parts. The temperament of the whole body is not a mixture of organ parts but a relational balance arising from juxtaposition. The passage thus distinguishes between the temperament of the whole body (personal temperament) and that of individual organs.

Key Terms
مزاج
عرض
اعتدال
مزاج شخصي
مزاج العضو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the heart's temperament exceeds its two limits according to the passage?
A) The heart becomes stronger
B) The heart ceases to be a heart
C) The heart becomes healthier
D) The heart's temperament adjusts

Key Takeaway
Both individuals and organs have a temperament breadth with two extremes and an optimal midpoint that must be maintained for proper function.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your temperament changes with age—have you honestly adjusted your spiritual and physical habits to match where you are now, or are you still living like a younger (or older) version of yourself? What is one area where you need to accept a new equilibrium?
?
The heart has a temperament that is uniquely its own, with limits that if exceeded it stops being the heart. What specific excess or deficiency in your life is pushing your heart beyond its proper range? What step will you take today to return toward the midpoint?
?
If the optimal state for Zayd is not a fixed point but shifts with time, how have you mistaken a past 'best state' for a permanent one? What would it mean for you to seek Allah's guidance for your current stage rather than clinging to a formula that worked years ago?

Hierarchy of Temperament Breadth: Species, Class, and Individual
Explains hierarchical inclusion of temperament breadths and excludes organ temperament.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the temperament breadth of a species encompasses the temperaments of its classes, and that of a class encompasses its individuals, and why the temperament of an organ is not included in this hierarchy.

Core Idea
Temperament breadth nests: species includes classes, classes include individuals.

Having established the breadth for individuals and organs, the author now generalizes to higher categories. He shows that the breadth of a species' temperament includes the temperaments of its classes, and the breadth of a class includes its individuals. He then explicitly excludes organ temperament from this hierarchy, explaining that whole body temperament is based on the balance of all organs, not a single organ.

Guided Path
1
Read the generalization from species to class to individual
Notice how the breadth flows downwards: species breadth contains class breadths, which contain individual breadths. This is because the purpose of the class is part of the purpose of the species.
2
Note the exclusion of organ temperament
The passage states that organ temperament is not included in these breadths. Try to guess why before reading on.
The next sentence gives the reason.
3
Understand the reason for exclusion
The whole body's temperament is considered as a balance of all organs when their hot, cold, moist, and dry qualities counteract each other. Therefore, it cannot be the temperament of a single organ.

Why is the temperament of an organ not included in the breadths of species, class, and individual?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The source excludes organ temperament because the whole is more than a part. Where in your life have you focused on a single 'organ'—a single goal, relationship, or skill—at the expense of your overall purpose? What would it mean to surrender that part for the sake of the whole?

Objection on Organ Temperament and Its Resolution
Presents and resolves an objection regarding the temperament of organs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that organs should have their own generic, specific, and individual temperament equilibrium, and the resolution that medicine considers whole body temperament as a relational balance of all organs without mixing of parts.

Core Idea
Organs are not omitted; they have their own equilibrium, but whole body temperament is a different concept.

A potential objection arises: since organs are also types of beings with classes and individuals, they should have their own hierarchy of temperament equilibrium (generic, specific, individual). The author responds that while this is true, the medical science focuses on the whole body's temperament as a balance of all organs' qualities, which is not a mixture but a relational quality from juxtaposition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection says that an organ is a type of being, so it should have its own generic, specific, and individual equilibrium, not be a separate division.
2
Read the beginning of the answer
The author agrees ('Yes'), but clarifies that medicine's perspective is different: it considers the whole body's equilibrium as a balance of all organs. This is why organ temperament is treated separately.
3
Understand the nature of whole body temperament
The passage explains that whole body temperament is not a mixture of organ parts but a relational quality arising from the juxtaposition of organs and their mutual influence. It balances heat/cold and moisture/dryness across the entire body.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Note the key point: personal temperament is a balance, not a mixture. This answers the objection by showing two different contexts: organ equilibrium vs. whole body equilibrium.

Thus it appears that the breadth of the temperament of the species includes the temperaments of its classes, because the purpose (3) of the class is part of the purpose (4) of the species, and the breadth of the temperament of the class includes the temperaments of its individuals, and the breadth of the temperament of the individual includes his temperaments in his states, and the temperament of an organ is not included in the aforementioned breadths (5), because they are taken with consideration of the entire body, insofar as it is a whole, when the hot organs are balanced with the cold, and the moist with the dry, so it is impossible for the temperament of the entire body to be the temperament of a single organ. For it is said: An organ is a type of types of beings, comprising classes comprising individuals, so it should be considered for it a generic, specific (6), and individual equilibrium, each relative to the internal and external, without making it a separate division (7) opposing them. We say: Yes, but they looked at that medicine (1) considers the states of the human body and its organs insofar as they are in equilibrium or outside of it, and the consideration of the body's temperament is only with consideration of the balance of its organs and their equality in temperament, such that the heat of what is hot among them, like the heart, balances the cold of what is cold among them, like the brain, and the dryness of what is dry among them, like bone, balances the moisture of what is moist among them, like the liver, such that if all the heat in the body is compared to all the cold in it, it is close (1) to equality, and likewise moisture with dryness. In summary, the resultant from the whole is close to real equilibrium. Then it is not hidden that here, i.e., in the temperament of the entire body, expressed as the personal temperament, there is no mingling of the parts (2) of the organs and their becoming small and mixing, but rather it is merely a position and a relation of some to others, or qualities that occur to all the organs from the influence of some on others by mere juxtaposition without mingling and mixing of parts.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of the breadth (عرض) of temperament (مزاج) across different levels: species, class, individual, and organ. It begins by noting that a person's temperament changes with age (youth, old age, childhood) and has a range with two extremes and a midpoint. Similarly, each organ, like the heart, has its own suitable temperament with limits. The author then generalizes: the breadth of the species' temperament encompasses the temperaments of its classes, and the breadth of a class encompasses its individuals. However, the temperament of an individual organ is not included in the earlier-mentioned breadths, because those consider the whole body as a balance of hot, cold, moist, and dry organs. An objection is raised: since an organ is also a type with classes and individuals, it should have its own generic, specific, and individual equilibrium. The answer clarifies that medicine (الطب) considers the whole body's equilibrium as a balance of all organs, not the mixing of parts. The temperament of the whole body is not a mixture of organ parts but a relational balance arising from juxtaposition. The passage thus distinguishes between the temperament of the whole body (personal temperament) and that of individual organs.

Key Terms
اعتدال نوعي
اعتدال صنفي
اعتدال شخصي
مزاج شخصي
تكافؤ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the objection, how is the temperament of the entire body determined?
A) By averaging the temperaments of all organs
B) By balancing the heat of hot organs with cold of cold organs, and moist with dry
C) By selecting the temperament of the most dominant organ
D) By mixing all organ parts together

Key Takeaway
Whole body temperament is a relational balance of all organs' qualities, not a mixture, which is why organ temperament is treated separately in medical theory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you focus more on perfecting individual aspects (like health, career, relationships) or on the overall balance between them? How might the lesson's emphasis on holistic temperament challenge your approach?
?
The author describes whole body temperament as a relation without mixing. Are there areas where you try to force a 'mixing' of opposites (e.g., work and rest, faith and reason) rather than allowing them to coexist through mutual influence? What would change if you aimed for relational balance?
?
The objection reflects a tendency to treat parts as separate subjects. When you face a conflict between different roles or duties, do you try to resolve each separately or look for the overall equilibrium? What practical step can you take today to see your own life's 'temperament' as a relational whole?

The Eight Deviations from Equilibrium Defended
Presents claim, objection, and answer regarding eight deviations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the number of deviations from equilibrium is limited to eight despite an objection that combinatorial calculations suggest eighty.

Core Idea
Equilibrium depends on ratio, not raw quantity, so deviations are only eight.

This passage examines a potential problem with the claim that there are only eight deviations from the balanced mixture. An objector argues that increasing or decreasing the four qualities in various combinations yields far more than eight. The response clarifies that equilibrium is about preserving a specific ratio between active and passive qualities, not about absolute quantities, thus defending the original count.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a claim, an objection, and an answer.
The passage uses a condensed style typical of kalam texts.
2
Understand the objection
The objector calculates eighty deviations by considering increase or decrease in one, two, three, or all four qualities. Work through the combinatorics to see how 80 is derived.
Single: 4 qualities × 2 directions = 8; Double: 6 pairs × 4 combos = 24; Triple: 4 triples × 8 = 32; Quadruple: 1 × 16 = 16; total = 80.
3
Analyze the answer
The answer explains that equilibrium is about the ratio between the two active qualities (heat and cold) and between the two passive qualities (moisture and dryness). As long as the ratio is preserved, equilibrium holds even if absolute quantities change. The example of 10:5 ratio shows why 12:6 and 8:4 are still equilibrium.
Only when the ratio changes do we get a true deviation, limiting the count to eight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (والخارج عن هذا الاعتدال أيضاً ينحصر في ثمانية. على قياس الحقيقي. واعترض بأن الخروج بالمتضادين (۳) ممكن ههنا بأن يزيدا أو ينقصا من القدر اللائق فيجوز الخروج بكيفية أو كيفيتين أو ثلاث أو أربع بحسب الزيادة والنقصان جمعاً وإفراداً يصير ثمانين . وأجيب : بأن معنى توفر القسط اللائق أن يكون بين الفاعلتين نسبة تليق بالممتزج، وكذا بين المنفعلتين، فما دامت النسبة محفوظة، فالاعتدال بحاله سواء زادت المقادير أو انتقصت كما إذا كان اللائق عشرة أجزاء من الحرارة، وخمسة من البرودة، فصارت الحرارة اثني عشر أو ثمانية والبرودة ستة أو أربعة، وإن صارت البرودة ستة، والحرارة أحد عشر فهو أبرد مما ينبغي لا أحر ، أو ثلاثة عشر فأحرلا
He said: 'And what is outside this equilibrium is also confined to eight, on the analogy of the real.' And it is objected: 'Departure by the two opposites (3) is possible here by their increase or decrease from the appropriate amount, so departure by one quality, two qualities, three, or four, according to increase or decrease, collectively or individually, becomes eighty.' And it is answered: 'The meaning of the availability of the appropriate portion is that there is a ratio between the two active [qualities] suitable for the thing mixed, and likewise between the two passive [qualities]; as long as the ratio is preserved, the equilibrium remains, whether the quantities increase or decrease, as when the suitable [amount] is ten parts of heat and five parts of cold, so the heat becomes twelve or eight and the cold six or four, and if the cold becomes six and the heat eleven, it is colder than it should be, not hotter, or thirteen, so hotter? not'.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage first states that deviations from the equilibrium (الاعتدال) are confined to eight types, following the pattern of the real equilibrium. It then presents an objection: because deviation can occur by increase or decrease of the two opposites (heat-cold, moisture-dryness) from the appropriate amount, considering all combinations for one, two, three, or four qualities with increase or decrease individually or collectively would yield eighty deviations (8 + 24 + 32 + 16 = 80). The answer clarifies that the 'appropriate portion' (القسط اللائق) requires a specific ratio between the active qualities (heat and cold) and between the passive qualities (moisture and dryness). As long as that ratio is maintained, the equilibrium is preserved even if the absolute quantities change. For example, if the suitable amount is 10 parts heat and 5 parts cold, then 12:6 and 8:4 both preserve the ratio 2:1 and thus are still equilibrium. Only when the ratio changes does a true deviation occur, which reduces the number to eight (presumably the four qualities each having excess or deficiency). The answer uses the example: if heat becomes 11 and cold 6, the ratio is not 2:1, so it is colder than appropriate; if heat becomes 13, it is hotter. Thus the number of deviations is defended as eight.

Key Terms
خروج عن الاعتدال
متضادان
قسط لائق
فاعلتان
منفعلتان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that any change in the quantities of heat, cold, moisture, or dryness produces a distinct deviation from equilibrium.
Correction: The passage shows that equilibrium depends on preserving the ratio between the two active and the two passive qualities. Changes that maintain the ratio (e.g., 10:5 to 12:6) are still equilibrium.

Checkpoint
How does the objection calculate eighty deviations from the equilibrium?
A) By considering four qualities each with increase or decrease, and all combinations of one to four qualities
B) By considering eight qualities with two extremes each
C) By multiplying four qualities by twenty
D) By adding the number of hot and cold organs

Key Takeaway
Equilibrium is defined by maintaining the proper ratio between active and passive qualities, so only when that ratio is disrupted does a true deviation occur, limiting the number to eight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your life do you keep adding more effort or deeds (quantity) without checking whether the 'ratio' with quality, intention, or balance with other duties is off? What is one concrete step you can take this week to restore that ratio?
?
When you face objections or criticism about your faith or conduct, do you get overwhelmed by the number of issues (like the objection of eighty), or do you seek the underlying principle (the 'ratio') to address the real problem? How will you train yourself to look for the root proportion before reacting?

Definition of Real Temperament and Its Nature
Author defines real temperament and explains its relational nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the real temperament of the body is, how balance of qualities is achieved, and why it is a relational state rather than a mixture.

Core Idea
Real temperament is a relational balance, not a blend of parts.

In this passage, the author explains the concept of real temperament (al-i'tidal al-haqiqi) in the human body. He describes how the four primary qualities are distributed across different organs to achieve overall balance. Most importantly, he clarifies that this temperament is not a physical mixing but a relational state arising from the mutual influence of organs by adjacency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the counterbalancing of qualities
Notice how the author contrasts cold organs (brain) with warm ones, dry organs (bone) with moist ones (liver), and explains that overall they are nearly equal.
The key is the comparison of totals across the whole body.
3
Note the clarification about no mixing
The author explicitly states that in personal temperament there is no blending of parts; it is merely a relation (wad') and attribution (idafa).
This distinguishes the concept from chemical mixing.
4
Reflect on the significance
Ask yourself: why does it matter that temperament is relational rather than material? How does this affect our understanding of health and balance?
It means temperament is an accidental quality, not a substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برودة ما هو بارد منها كالدماغ ، ويبوسة ما هو يابس منها كالعظم تعادل رطوبة ما هو رطب منها كالكبد ، بحيث إذا نسب جميع ما في البدن من الحرارة إلى جميع ما فيه من البرودة كان قريباً من التساوي، وكذا الرطوبة مع اليبوسة. وبالجملة يكون الحاصل من المجموع قريباً من الاعتدال الحقيقي. ثم لاخفاء في أنه ليس ههنا أعني في مزاج جملة البدن المعبر بالمزاج الشخصي، اختلاط أجزاء الأعضاء، وتصغرها وتمارسها ، وكأنه مجرد وضع ، وإضافة للبعض إلى البعض أو كيفيات تحصل لجميع الأعضاء من جهة تأثر بعضها من البعض بمجرد المجاورة من غير امتزاج واختلاط للأجزاء.
The coldness of what is cold among them, such as the brain, and the dryness of what is dry among them, such as bone, counterbalance the moisture of what is moist among them, such as the liver, so that if all the heat in the body is compared to all the cold in it, it is close to equality, and likewise moisture with dryness. In summary, the result of the whole is close to real moderation. Then it is clear that here—that is, in the temperament of the entire body, called the personal temperament—there is no mixing of the parts of the organs, nor their composition and interpretation?; rather it is merely a relation and attribution of some to others, or qualities that arise in all organs due to some being affected by others merely by adjacency, without blending and mixing of the parts.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage defines the real temperament (al-i'tidal al-haqiqi) of the whole body. The author explains that the four primary qualities—heat, cold, moisture, dryness—are distributed among different organs (e.g., brain is cold, bone is dry, liver is moist) such that when the total heat in the body is compared to the total cold, they are nearly equal, and similarly for moisture and dryness. The overall result is close to perfect balance. Then he clarifies an important nuance: in the personal temperament (mizaj shakhsi), there is no actual blending of the parts of the organs; rather, it is merely a relational state (wad') and attribution (idafa) of some organs to others, or qualities that arise in the whole body due to mutual influence by mere adjacency, without any real mixture of parts. This distinguishes the concept of temperament from chemical blending.

Key Terms
اعتدال حقيقي
مزاج شخصي
كيفيات
مجرد وضع وإضافة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Temperament involves actual mixing of the four primary qualities or humors in the body.
Correction: The author clarifies that the personal temperament is merely a relational and attributive state resulting from adjacency, not a real blending of parts.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the nature of the personal temperament (al-mizaj al-shakhsi)?
A) It involves actual mixing of the four elements in the body.
B) It is a relational and attributive quality without real blending of parts.
C) It is identical to the equal balance of the four qualities in each organ.
D) It is a physical mixture of the four humors in the blood.

Key Takeaway
Real temperament is the nearly balanced result of mutual influence among organs, not a physical mixture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author describes the body's temperament as a near balance of qualities achieved through mutual influence without mixing. Could it be that your spiritual life also needs a similar balancing of inner qualities — not by removing parts of yourself, but by bringing them into relation with each other under the guidance of revelation? Which quality in you is out of balance, and what step can you take today to realign it?
?
If the temperament of your body is simply a relation and not a physical blend, then your identity is not a mixture of random parts but a relation to your Creator. How does this shift your understanding of who you are? What one commitment can you make to strengthen that relation right now?
